# Integrating Gemini Query Enhancement into Aluna Africaâ€™s RAG Stack

**Proposal Objective:** Incorporate the techniques from the *â€œGemini RAG Recipe with Query Enhancementâ€* (KDnuggets, April 2025) into Aluna Africaâ€™s multi-agent AI stack. We will outline how to adapt Gemini-based query enhancement (QE), hypothetical document generation, and reranking within Robâ€™s hybrid local/cloud RAG system â€“ which currently uses AnythingLLM, OpenWebUI, and local storage (F:\AI_RAM) â€“ to improve retrieval accuracy and answer quality. This proposal includes a step-by-step implementation plan, mapping Gemini model variants (1.5 Flash vs. 1.5 Pro) to agent roles (Bonny, Meneâ€™, Memory Agent), code snippets for key integrations, and fallback options if Gemini integration is limited.

## Introduction

LLMs are powerful but prone to **hallucination** â€“ confidently generating incorrect information. **Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG)** addresses this by providing the LLM with external knowledge relevant to the queryã€4â€ L54-L61ã€‘. The KDnuggets article demonstrates a local RAG â€œrecipeâ€ using **Googleâ€™s Gemini** models and a local vector database to ground the LLMâ€™s responsesã€4â€ L42-L46ã€‘ã€5â€ L83-L87ã€‘. Key to this recipe is **query enhancement**: before searching the knowledge base, the userâ€™s query is refined and expanded to better match relevant documentsã€5â€ L89-L97ã€‘. This includes **LLM-driven query rewriting** and **Hypothetical Document Embedding (HyDE)**, where a fake answer is generated and used as a search vectorã€5â€ L89-L97ã€‘. The result is a more precise retrieval of supporting facts, greatly reducing hallucinations in the final answer.

Aluna Africaâ€™s AI stack (â€œRobâ€™s hybrid RAG systemâ€) already blends local and cloud resources for RAG. Our goal is to seamlessly integrate Geminiâ€™s query enhancement techniques into this stack to boost its performance. We will ensure compatibility with existing components (AnythingLLMâ€™s agent framework, OpenWebUI for local models, LanceDB/Chroma for vectors) and assign appropriate Gemini model variants to each agent role for an optimal balance of speed and accuracy. By the end of this plan, the multi-agent system (agents **Bonny**, **Meneâ€™**, and **Memory Agent**) will collaborate more effectively â€“ producing highly accurate answers grounded in relevant context, with minimal hallucinations.

## Overview of the Gemini RAG Recipe

The *Gemini RAG Recipe* outlines a straightforward but powerful RAG pipeline using a local vector store plus Googleâ€™s Gemini API services. In summary, the method involves:

- **Local Knowledge Base & Vector Store:** Documents (e.g. a PDF) are ingested, chunked, and stored as embeddings in a local vector database (ChromaDB in the example)ã€5â€ L83-L87ã€‘. This means all data remains on the local machine, ensuring privacy, while enabling fast similarity search. (We can substitute **LanceDB** for Chroma; more on that later.)
- **Gemini for Embeddings and Generation:** The pipeline uses Googleâ€™s Generative AI API (Gemini family models) for both embedding generation and text generationã€5â€ L83-L87ã€‘. You need a Google API key to access these modelsã€5â€ L83-L87ã€‘. In the example, the `text-embedding-004` model produces vector embeddings for each chunk of text, and a **Gemini 1.5** model is used for all LLM tasks (rewriting queries, generating the hypothetical document, and answering)ã€9â€ L175-L183ã€‘ã€6â€ L37-L45ã€‘.
- **Query Rewriting (QE):** Before retrieval, the userâ€™s query is *reformulated by an LLM* to be more specific and detailedã€5â€ L89-L97ã€‘. For example, â€œWhat is residual markets in insurance?â€ might be rewritten to â€œIn insurance, what does the term â€˜residual marketsâ€™ refer to, and how do such markets function?â€ â€“ adding context that helps find relevant info. The Gemini model (1.5 Flash in the articleâ€™s code) is prompted to perform this reformulationã€9â€ L223-L230ã€‘. *â€œQuery rewriting is a technique for improving the query passed for retrieval by making it more specific and detailedâ€*ã€5â€ L89-L97ã€‘.
- **Hypothetical Document Embedding (HyDE):** After rewriting, a **hypothetical answer** is generated for the query (again using the LLM)ã€10â€ L243-L252ã€‘. This is essentially the modelâ€™s best guess at a detailed answer, treated as a pseudo-document. By embedding this hypothetical answer and searching the vector DB with it, we often retrieve actual documents that are semantically similar to the answer we needã€10â€ L258-L267ã€‘. *â€œHyDEâ€¦ transforms queries into hypothetical documents containing potential answersâ€¦ intended to bridge the semantic gap between queries and documents in vector spaceâ€*ã€5â€ L89-L97ã€‘. This step can recall relevant pieces of text even if they donâ€™t share the exact phrasing of the userâ€™s question.
- **Document Retrieval:** The vector database is queried using the enhanced query representation (either the rewritten query embedding or the hypothetical document embedding or both). Top-$k$ matching chunks are returned as contextã€10â€ L258-L267ã€‘. In the recipe, ChromaDB is used for similarity search, storing and retrieving chunks by their embeddingsã€9â€ L192-L200ã€‘ã€9â€ L201-L207ã€‘. By default, Chroma stores data locally on diskã€9â€ L185-L193ã€‘, which aligns with a local/hybrid deployment.
- **(Optional) Reranking:** Although not explicitly detailed in the KDnuggets article, an additional rerank step can be applied on the retrieved chunks. This uses a model to assess which chunks most directly answer the query, reordering them. Googleâ€™s Gemini API includes a **Ranking service** that *â€œtakes a list of documents and reranks those documents based on how relevant they are to a queryâ€*, offering more precise relevance scoring than raw vector similarityã€30â€ L1-L4ã€‘. Weâ€™ll consider this as an enhancement to the base recipe.
- **Generative Answering:** Finally, the top retrieved content is fed into the LLM (along with the original user question) to produce an answerã€10â€ L276-L284ã€‘. Because the answer is constructed with reference to real documents, it stays factual (the model can cite the provided context rather than making something up). In the example, the Gemini modelâ€™s answer about â€œresidual marketsâ€ was accurate and drawn from the insurance handbook contentã€23â€ L360-L369ã€‘ã€23â€ L372-L380ã€‘. The article confirms that *â€œusing the Gemini language model, ChromaDB vector database, and query enhancement techniquesâ€* one can develop a reliable RAG systemã€23â€ L382-L388ã€‘.

Overall, this recipe is highly relevant to Aluna Africaâ€™s needs: itâ€™s designed to run on a local machine (all data storage and processing is local, with the only cloud dependency being the calls to Gemini models), and it significantly improves answer quality by enhancing the retrieval step. Next, weâ€™ll discuss how this fits with Robâ€™s current hybrid system and then dive into a concrete implementation plan.

## Compatibility with Robâ€™s Hybrid Local/Cloud RAG System

Robâ€™s current setup comprises **AnythingLLM** (an open-source all-in-one RAG and agent orchestration tool), **OpenWebUI** (for running local LLMs with a web API), and a local data store (the `F:\AI_RAM` directory). The integration of Gemini-based enhancements is *highly compatible* with this architecture:

- **Local Vector Database:** AnythingLLM uses a local vector store to index and retrieve document embeddings. Notably, AnythingLLMâ€™s default vector DB is **LanceDB**, a lightweight local vector databaseã€24â€ L53-L61ã€‘. (It actually ships with LanceDB out-of-the-boxã€24â€ L53-L61ã€‘, though it can likely be configured to use others.) This is analogous to the recipeâ€™s use of ChromaDB â€“ both are local, persistent vector stores with Python APIs for adding and querying embeddings. We can continue using LanceDB on the `F:\AI_RAM` path or switch to Chroma if needed; the Gemini recipeâ€™s logic will work with either with minimal changes. In short, the **â€œlocal vs. hosted vector databaseâ€** choice mentioned in the recipe is already aligned to â€œlocalâ€ in Robâ€™s systemã€4â€ L69-L77ã€‘.
- **Document Ingestion and Chunking:** Alunaâ€™s pipeline for ingesting documents (PDFs, text, etc.) can remain largely the same. The KDnuggets implementation uses PyPDF2 and text splitters to chunk documentsã€5â€ L123-L131ã€‘ã€5â€ L151-L159ã€‘, similar to typical RAG setups. If AnythingLLM already has a mechanism for uploading documents and chunking them (which it does, via â€œworkspacesâ€ for documents), we can hook into that. The chunk size/overlap parameters can be adjusted as needed (the recipe uses 500 characters with 50 overlapã€9â€ L157-L165ã€‘). So our ingestion is compatible â€“ weâ€™ll just augment what happens *after* chunking.
- **Local LLM (OpenWebUI) vs. Gemini Cloud LLM:** Currently, the system likely uses a local LLM (via OpenWebUI, maybe running a model like Llama 2 or similar) to answer queries. With Gemini integration, we introduce calls to a *cloud LLM* (Gemini) for certain tasks. This hybrid approach (local retrieval + cloud LLM) is exactly what the recipe illustratesã€5â€ L83-L87ã€‘. We can mix and match: use Gemini for query processing and possibly final answer generation, while still having the local LLM as a fallback or for less critical queries. AnythingLLM supports â€œenterpriseâ€ providers (OpenAI, Azure, etc.), and by extension we can configure it to call Googleâ€™s API as a custom provider. The open architecture of AnythingLLM means we can extend it â€“ for example, by creating a custom agent that routes to Gemini for specific prompts. In practice, weâ€™ll use the **Google Generative AI SDK** (or REST API) within our pipeline code to call Gemini models; as long as the system has internet access and an API key, this is straightforward.
- **No Conflict in Components:** Using Gemini doesnâ€™t disrupt existing components. The vector store remains local (so data control is preserved), and weâ€™re not removing OpenWebUI â€“ it can co-exist. We may repurpose the roles of agents (e.g. Bonny might switch from using the local model to calling Gemini for final answers, or Meneâ€™ might be a new agent specifically for query enhancement). Because AnythingLLM allows multiple agents and even agent chaining, we can insert the Gemini-enhanced steps as needed. Also, F:\AI_RAM (if itâ€™s where embeddings or DB files are stored) will continue to be used; integrating Chroma might store data in its own folder structure, but LanceDB can directly use that path for its database file.
- **Data Privacy Consideration:** In a hybrid setup, sending data to Gemini (a cloud service) means that portion of data is exposed externally. We should be mindful of what we send. Query texts and the content of the chunks we embed/query with Gemini will go to Googleâ€™s servers. If the data is sensitive and privacy is paramount, we might choose to only send queries (which are user-provided anyway) and keep document embedding local. However, given that Robâ€™s system already contemplated cloud usage (OpenAI, etc.), using Googleâ€™s API is probably acceptable under the same terms. We will mention mitigations (like only using Gemini for queries, not document text) in the fallback section, if needed.

In summary, **Geminiâ€™s RAG recipe is naturally aligned with a hybrid local/cloud architecture**. It was literally designed for â€œlocal vector DB + hosted LLMâ€ã€4â€ L69-L77ã€‘, which maps onto Alunaâ€™s â€œlocal data + optional cloud AIâ€ setup. The main tasks are to wire up the Google API calls within the AnythingLLM framework and to adjust any configurations (like using LanceDB instead of Chroma, which is trivial). With that compatibility confirmed, letâ€™s move on to a concrete implementation plan.

## Implementation Plan: Gemini Query Enhancement in the RAG Pipeline

We will integrate Geminiâ€™s Query Enhancement and reranking step-by-step into the Aluna Africa RAG pipeline. Below is a structured plan with actionable steps, corresponding to the flow of a typical query handling in the system:

### **1. Document Ingestion & Embedding Setup**

**Action:** Ensure documents are loaded, split, and embedded in the vector database. 

- *Chunking:* Continue using the existing mechanism (likely AnythingLLMâ€™s importer or a LangChain text splitter) to break documents into manageable chunks (e.g. 500 tokens with some overlap). This was done in the recipe with a RecursiveCharacterTextSplitterã€9â€ L157-L165ã€‘, but since Alunaâ€™s stack already handles chunking when adding docs to the knowledge base, we can stick with that. Just verify the chunk size is reasonable (the recipeâ€™s 500 characters is a good default).
- *Embedding Model:* We have two options for generating embeddings for each chunk:
  - **Option A: Use Geminiâ€™s embedding model.** The KDnuggets recipe calls `genai.embed_content(model="models/text-embedding-004", content=chunk)` to get a vector for each chunkã€9â€ L175-L183ã€‘. This corresponds to Googleâ€™s latest text embedding model (likely a PaLM/Gemini embedding model). Using this ensures the embeddings are in the same vector space as the query embeddings weâ€™ll get from Gemini later, maximizing retrieval performance. We would need to call this for each chunk (the example loops through each and appends resultsã€9â€ L173-L181ã€‘). The embedding dimensionality will be whatever `text-embedding-004` provides (we can store it as a list of floats in LanceDB/Chroma).
  - **Option B: Use the existing local embedder.** AnythingLLM by default might be using a local embedding model (perhaps Instructor or SentenceTransformer) to vectorize documents. We could keep that to avoid using API calls for ingestion. However, note that if we use a different model for documents vs. queries, there could be a slight vector space mismatch. Itâ€™s often best if the same embedding model (or at least similarly trained) is used for both documents and queries. Since we plan to use Gemini for query embedding (especially with HyDE, where we embed the hypothetical answer using Gemini), using Geminiâ€™s own embed model for documents is recommended for compatibility. 
- *Storing in Vector DB:* After embedding, store the chunks and their embeddings in the vector database:
  - For **ChromaDB:** The articleâ€™s code simply uses the local client, creates a collection, and adds documents with embeddingsã€9â€ L192-L200ã€‘ã€9â€ L201-L207ã€‘. Chroma will save this in a local directory (by default `.chromadb/` in the working directory). This could be set to F:\AI_RAM if we want.
  - For **LanceDB:** AnythingLLM already likely created a LanceDB for its documents (as noted, it â€œprovided LanceDB vector storeâ€ out of the boxã€24â€ L53-L61ã€‘). We can either use that existing instance or create a new one for the enhanced pipeline. LanceDB usage is also straightforward: connect to a directory (which could be `F:\AI_RAM\lancedb`), get or create a table (collection) for our knowledge domain, and insert records containing the embedding and metadata. For example, using LanceDBâ€™s Python API, it would look like:
    ```python
    import lancedb
    db = lancedb.connect("F:/AI_RAM/my_vectors")
    table = db.create_table("knowledge_chunks")
    # Assuming chunks_list and embeddings_list are prepared:
    data = [{"id": f"chunk_{i}", "text": chunk, "embedding": embedding} 
            for i, (chunk, embedding) in enumerate(zip(chunks_list, embeddings_list))]
    table.add(data)  # LanceDB will handle creating an index
    ```
    This is analogous to the Chroma snippet (Chromaâ€™s `collection.add` was used similarly with ids, documents, and embeddingsã€9â€ L199-L207ã€‘).
  - In either case, verify the vector store is persisted (so we donâ€™t need to re-embed every restart) and that it can be queried for nearest neighbors by embedding.

**Outcome:** All documents are indexed in the local vector DB. The system is ready to accept queries and perform retrieval. With embedding done (either via Gemini or local), the next steps focus on query-time enhancements.

### **2. Query Rewriting with Gemini (Query Enhancement)**

**Action:** Intercept user queries and rewrite them using a Gemini model to improve clarity and specificity for retrieval.

- **Where it fits:** In the normal flow, a userâ€™s question would directly be converted to an embedding and used to query the DB. Here, we insert a step: when Bonny (the main agent) receives a user query, it should first call a **Query Enhancer agent** (or function) â€“ which could be represented by the agent *Meneâ€™* â€“ that returns a refined query. This refined query will then be used for the actual retrieval. This could be configured as an internal chain: Bonny -> Meneâ€™ -> Memory Agent.
- **Using Gemini for rewriting:** We leverage Gemini 1.5â€™s language model for this task. The KDnuggets recipe provides a prompt template and sample code for query rewriting:
  - The prompt instructs the model: *â€œYou are an AI assistant tasked with reformulating user queries to improve retrieval in a RAG systemâ€¦ Given the original query, rewrite it to be more specific and detailedâ€¦â€*ã€9â€ L213-L221ã€‘. This guides Gemini to output a single, improved query.
  - In code, they do:
    ```python
    model = genai.GenerativeModel("gemini-1.5-flash")
    response = model.generate_content(query_rewrite_template.format(original_query=orig_query))
    rewritten_query = response.text
    ``` 
    ã€9â€ L223-L230ã€‘. We can adapt this directly. Here `genai` is the Google generative AI Python library, which we would have configured with our API key earlierã€9â€ L169-L177ã€‘.
  - We will use **Gemini 1.5 Flash** for this operation. Flash is a smaller, faster model suitable for â€œlower-intelligence tasksâ€ like simple transformationsã€28â€ L1-L4ã€‘. Rewriting a query doesnâ€™t require heavy reasoning, just linguistic reformulation, so Flash-8B is sufficient (and cost-effective).
- **Integration in AnythingLLM:** There are a few ways to integrate this:
  - Easiest might be to implement this logic in the **Memory Agentâ€™s retrieval function**. i.e., when Memory Agent gets a query, it first calls `rewrite_query()` internally before doing the search. If AnythingLLMâ€™s Memory Agent code is accessible, we can insert these few lines with the API call. This keeps it transparent to the rest of the system (Bonny still sends the original query to Memory Agent, but Memory Agent uses the rewritten query behind the scenes).
  - Alternatively, implement a dedicated **Meneâ€™ agent** whose sole job is to take an input query and output a rewritten query. Bonny can then be configured to ask Meneâ€™ to preprocess the question. This might be more complex to orchestrate, but given the multi-agent capabilities, itâ€™s plausible. The advantage is it cleanly separates concerns (Meneâ€™ = query refiner). We would create Meneâ€™ with a prompt pattern similar to the above template (which could be set as its system prompt for every conversation turn).
  - If direct integration into AnythingLLMâ€™s chain is difficult, we can manage it in a custom pipeline script that wraps around AnythingLLMâ€™s API. For instance, a small Python service could receive queries, call Gemini to rewrite, then call AnythingLLMâ€™s retrieval with the rewritten query. However, since the user specifically mentioned multi-agent, integrating within that framework is preferable.
- **Example:** User asks: â€œHow does our companyâ€™s health insurance handle pre-existing conditions?â€ â€“ The query enhancer (via Gemini) might rewrite this to: *â€œIn the context of [Our Company] health insurance policy, what are the rules and provisions regarding coverage of pre-existing medical conditions?â€*. This added specificity (company context, and phrasing as a direct question about provisions) will likely match the phrasing in an internal policy document better.
- **Verification:** We should log the original and rewritten queries for transparency. The recipe does `logger.info(f"Rewritten Query: {rewritten_query}")`ã€27â€ L7-L10ã€‘. This helps us ensure the rewriting is reasonable. If the rewritten query somehow changes the meaning, we might choose to fall back to the original or refine the prompt template.

By implementing query rewriting, we leverage *Geminiâ€™s language understanding to clarify user intent*, which increases the chance that the subsequent vector search will hit the right information. As the article noted, this is an easy enhancement that â€œyou can later adapt and use in your own projectsâ€ã€23â€ L376-L384ã€‘ â€“ exactly what we are doing here.

### **3. Hypothetical Document Generation (HyDE) & Enhanced Retrieval**

**Action:** Use the HyDE technique with Gemini to further enrich the query representation before querying the vector database.

- **Generate a Hypothetical Document:** Once we have a (possibly rewritten) query, we ask Gemini to produce a detailed answer to that query *as if it had all the information*. Essentially, we prompt the LLM: *â€œGiven the question X, generate a hypothetical document (several paragraphs) that directly answers this question in depth.â€*ã€10â€ L243-L252ã€‘. The KDnuggets code does exactly this in the `HyDERetriever.generate_hypothetical_document` method:
  ```python
  hyde_prompt = """Given the question '{query}', generate a hypothetical document 
  that directly answers this question. The document should be detailed and in-depth.
  The document size should be approximately {chunk_size} characters."""
  model = genai.GenerativeModel("gemini-1.5-flash")
  response = model.generate_content(hyde_prompt.format(query=query, chunk_size=self.chunk_size))
  hypo_doc = response.text
  ```
  ã€10â€ L243-L252ã€‘ã€10â€ L253-L261ã€‘. We will replicate this logic. We continue using **Gemini 1.5 Flash** here, because generating a few hundred characters of text is well within its capabilities, and Flash will do it quickly. The `chunk_size` parameter can be set similar to our document chunk length (e.g., 500 characters) so that the hypothetical answer is roughly one chunk long (this makes its embedding comparable to actual chunk embeddings).
- **Embed the Hypothetical Document:** Next, we embed the generated hypothetical text into a vector. If we decided in step 1 to use Gemini for embeddings, we do that here as well (`genai.embed_content`). If we stuck with a local embedder, we run the hypothetical text through the same embedder. The result is a vector in the same dimension as our document vectors.
- **Query Vector DB with Hypothesis Vector:** We then perform a nearest-neighbor search in our vector database using this hypothetical documentâ€™s embedding as the query vector. Essentially, weâ€™re asking: *find me real documents that are similar to this imagined answer*. Because the imagined answer likely uses terminology and context that directly relate to the actual answer, this often retrieves the most relevant chunks from our knowledge base, even if the original user query was phrased differently. The recipeâ€™s `HyDERetriever.retrieve` method shows this process:
  ```python
  hypothetical_embedding = generate_embeddings([hypothetical_doc])[0]
  results = collection.query(query_embeddings=[hypothetical_embedding], n_results=k)
  similar_docs = results["documents"][0]
  ```
  ã€10â€ L258-L267ã€‘. Weâ€™ll adapt that to LanceDB/Chroma. In LanceDB, for example, if we have the `table` object, we might do:
  ```python
  hits = table.search(hypo_vector).limit(k).to_df()
  similar_docs = hits["text"].tolist()
  ```
  (The exact API depends on LanceDBâ€™s client, but it should support a search returning the closest vectors.)
- **Use Rewritten Query vs. Original:** In the recipe, they apply HyDE after the query rewriting. We should follow that: use the rewritten query text as the input for hypothetical document generation. This likely yields an even more on-point hypothetical answer. (Alternatively, one could generate from the original query. We could even try both and combine results, but that may be overkill. Rewritten query should be fine.)
- **Fallback if no Hypo Doc:** If for some reason Gemini cannot generate the hypothetical doc (API error, etc.), we should fall back to a standard retrieval: i.e., embed the (rewritten) query itself and query the DB. The system should not fail entirely without HyDE â€“ it just might retrieve less relevant info.
- **Integration in Memory Agent:** We can integrate this in the Memory Agentâ€™s logic. Instead of a direct `vector_search(query)`, we do:
  1. `rewritten_q = rewrite_query(user_q)` (from step 2),
  2. `hypo_doc = generate_hypo_doc(rewritten_q)`,
  3. `hypo_emb = embed(hypo_doc)`,
  4. `results = vectordb.search(hypo_emb)`.
  Return `results` (plus optionally the hypothetical doc text if we want to log it or even include it in context).
- **Results:** The Memory Agent will return, say, the top *k* chunks (with *k* maybe 3 or 5 as set in the system) that are most similar to the hypothetical answer. According to the article, *â€œUsing both query rewriting and HyDE, we should be able to improve our RAG resultsâ€*ã€10â€ L270-L278ã€‘ â€“ these techniques combined yield much more relevant context for the next stage.

For example, if the question was about â€œpre-existing conditionsâ€ in our policy, the hypothetical document Gemini generates might explicitly talk about clauses for pre-existing conditions, waiting periods, exclusions, etc. When embedded, that will match actual policy text on those topics, even if the userâ€™s query didnâ€™t use the exact same wording as the policy. This means the Memory Agent could retrieve the specific paragraph from the policy document that lists how pre-existing conditions are handled, which is exactly what we need to answer the user.

### **4. Post-Retrieval Reranking (Gemini-Native or Local)**

**Action:** Optionally, refine the ordered list of retrieved documents using a reranking mechanism (potentially Gemini-based) to ensure the most relevant information is prioritized.

- **Why Rerank?:** Vector similarity search is good, but not perfect. It might return a chunk that is topically similar to the query but doesnâ€™t actually answer it, while a slightly lower-ranked chunk contains the precise answer. Reranking means analyzing the candidate chunks with respect to the query and sorting them by *actual relevance*. This is typically done with a more powerful model (like a cross-encoder or an LLM) that takes the query and a chunk and produces a relevance score.
- **Geminiâ€™s Ranking API:** Google offers a dedicated Ranking API as part of its generative AI tools. *â€œThe ranking API takes a list of documents and reranks those documents based on how well each answers a given queryâ€*, providing more precise relevance scores than embedding similarityã€30â€ L1-L4ã€‘. This is an attractive option because itâ€™s likely optimized and faster than using a full LLM to do the same. We can use this by sending the top *N* results (maybe N=5 or 10) from the vector search along with the query, and getting back a reordered list or relevance scores.
  - Implementation: The Vertex AI Python SDK (or REST endpoints) can be used. For instance, using the `generativeai` library, there might be a method like `genai.rank(query=query_text, documents=[doc1, doc2, ...])`. If not, the Vertex AI SDK provides a `MatchServiceClient` or `RankingServiceClient` where you construct a request with the query and documents and call `client.rank()`ã€18â€ L7-L10ã€‘. Weâ€™d have to integrate our Google credentials with this service (likely the same API key if it has permissions, or a separate service account).
  - If we use this, itâ€™s a cloud call for each query, but with a fixed small number of documents â€“ which is usually fine latency-wise.
- **LLM-based Reranking:** If the formal API is not accessible, we can simply use Gemini (or even the local LLM) in a prompt to achieve reranking. For example, we could prompt Gemini 1.5 Flash or Pro with: *â€œHere is a question and some excerpts from documents. Rank the excerpts by how relevant they are to answering the question.â€* and then list the chunks. The model can output an ordering or scores. This is essentially doing the same with an LLM instruction. The KDnuggets article didnâ€™t explicitly do this, but itâ€™s a logical extension (and Googleâ€™s own docs mention an â€œLLM rerankerâ€ as an approachã€15â€ L584-L593ã€‘).
  - Alternatively, we can ask the LLM that will answer the question to *choose the most relevant bits* as part of its answering (like a chain-of-thought where it decides which info is needed). However, it might be cleaner to do an explicit rerank step.
- **Integration:** We can integrate reranking right after retrieval:
  - Memory Agent, after getting `similar_docs`, can call a reranker. It would output, say, an ordered list (or just pick the top 3 among the 5 if we want to truncate).
  - If using Geminiâ€™s rank API, this could be another call out to Google. If using an LLM approach, it could be done with the same Gemini Flash model. If using a local cross-encoder (fallback scenario), that would be done locally.
  - We then take the reranked top results as the final context.
- **Latency/Cost Note:** We should consider that weâ€™ve already made 2 API calls (rewrite and HyDE generation) for one query, plus an embed call. Adding a rerank call is a third. If this is too slow, we might choose to only do it for certain queries (e.g., if the top vectors have close similarity scores and weâ€™re not sure which is best). We can also adjust `k` to a smaller number to mitigate this. For critical use-cases (where accuracy is paramount), reranking is worth it. Google notes *â€œRerankers introduce latency, which increases with the number of processed contextsâ€*ã€15â€ L600-L608ã€‘ â€“ so keep the list small.
- **Example:** Suppose our vector search returned 5 chunks about â€œpre-existing conditionsâ€: one might be a general definition, another might be an example, and a couple might be the actual policy clauses. Reranking can push the policy clause chunk to position 1 because it directly answers the question about â€œhow does the policy handle itâ€ (it might contain wording like â€œpre-existing conditions are covered after X monthsâ€). The ranking API would detect that this chunk *best matches the query intent* (covering a pre-existing condition handling) as opposed to just containing the keywords.

If implementing this, we ensure the answer agent (Bonny) always gets the *most relevant* context first, which increases the quality of its response. That leads us to the final answering step.

### **5. Answer Generation with Gemini (Final Response)**

**Action:** Generate the final answer using the top retrieved context, ideally using a stronger Gemini model for better quality.

- **Compose the Prompt with Context:** We now have (after reranking or directly from retrieval) a set of relevant text chunks. We concatenate them into a single context string (perhaps separated by delimiters or newlines). Then we form a prompt to the LLM that includes this context and the original user question. The recipe used a simple template: **â€œContext: {context}\n\nQuestion: {query}\n\nAnswer:â€** and fed that to the modelã€10â€ L276-L284ã€‘. We can use a similar format. The context may include multiple discrete pieces of text; itâ€™s good to clearly delineate them (some use bullet points or numbered sources). 
  - We might also include a brief system instruction like: *â€œUse the context to answer the question. If the context is insufficient, say you donâ€™t know. Do not fabricate information.â€* â€“ This reinforces the grounding. Since the userâ€™s stack might prefer answers that integrate sources or at least are verifiably from content, such instructions help.
- **Select the Gemini Model:** For this final step, we recommend using **Gemini 1.5 Pro** (or a similarly capable model) rather than Flash. The final answer often requires reasoning, synthesis, and possibly a longer response. Gemini 1.5 Pro is described as *â€œoptimized for a wide-range of reasoning tasksâ€* and can handle very large inputs (up to 2,000 pages of text)ã€29â€ L1-L4ã€‘. While we wonâ€™t feed it that much, this means it can easily take several documents worth of context without issues. Its stronger reasoning ability will allow it to draw connections between multiple context chunks and formulate a coherent answer. In contrast, the Flash model might be somewhat limited in complex reasoning due to its smaller sizeã€28â€ L1-L4ã€‘. So:
  - We call: `model = genai.GenerativeModel("gemini-1.5-pro")` and then `model.generate_content(full_prompt)` to get the answerã€6â€ L49-L52ã€‘. The output `.text` will be the answer string.
  - If cost or access to 1.5 Pro is an issue, 1.5 Flash can be used here as well (the article did use flash even for answer generationã€10â€ L277-L284ã€‘). However, for Alunaâ€™s use case (particularly if Bonny is the flagship answer agent), using the best available model makes sense to maximize answer quality and correctness.
- **Incorporating Agents:** In practice, this step is what Bonny (the main agent) would do. After Memory Agent returns the context, Bonny formulates the question+context prompt and calls the LLM. We can implement Bonny such that it uses the Gemini model via API instead of the local OpenWebUI model when high quality is desired. Since AnythingLLM allows multiple LLM backends, Bonny could even dynamically decide: e.g., use Gemini Pro for complex queries or when internet is available, otherwise use local.
- **Answer Format:** The answer can be a normal paragraph or a list, depending on the question. If sources need to be cited, we could even prompt the model to include which document (maybe by an ID if we had it in metadata) it got the info from. That could be a future enhancement (requiring the model to output citations).
- **Example:** Continuing our running example, the context from Memory Agent might include a snippet like: *â€œâ€¦pre-existing conditions: Coverage for any pre-existing condition will commence after a waiting period of 12 months from the policy start dateâ€¦â€* along with some other bits. The prompt to Gemini Pro would include that, and the question *â€œHow does our health insurance handle pre-existing conditions?â€*. Gemini Pro would then answer along the lines of: *â€œOur companyâ€™s health insurance policy covers pre-existing conditions, but only after a waiting period of 12 months from the start of the policy. In other words, any medical condition you had before enrolling will not be covered during the first year of coverage. After that period, the condition becomes eligible for coverage.â€* â€“ This answer directly uses the content from the context (grounded, factual, and precise). Bonny would present this to the user.
- **Multi-turn considerations:** If the conversation continues, we might feed the last user question plus some prior context. The memory agent can be used each turn for retrieval if needed. Bonny (with Gemini) is capable of maintaining conversational context if we include it, but since we have explicit memory agent, we likely always re-retrieve from knowledge base as needed. The system can accumulate chat history separately (perhaps summarized by a memory agent or stored vectorially, which is another topic).

At this stage, we have a complete pipeline: **User Query -> (Gemini Flash) Query Rewrite -> (Gemini Flash) Hypothetical Answer -> Vector Search (LanceDB) -> Top Docs -> (Gemini Pro) Final Answer.** This matches the recipeâ€™s spirit, with the addition of reranking and model differentiation for efficiency.

## Enhancing Multi-Agent Collaboration with Gemini QE

With the technical steps laid out, letâ€™s map them onto the **multi-agent workflow** involving **Bonny**, **Meneâ€™**, and **Memory Agent**. Each agentâ€™s role can be augmented or adjusted to incorporate Gemini-driven enhancements:

- **Bonny (Primary Answer Agent):** Bonny is the interface to the user, responsible for delivering the final answer. In the enhanced pipeline, Bonnyâ€™s role is to orchestrate the other agents and compile the answer. Instead of directly answering using a local model, Bonny will delegate the *information-gathering* tasks to Memory Agent (and Meneâ€™) and then use the **Gemini Pro** model to generate the answer. Bonny essentially becomes a *â€œcontrollerâ€* and final *â€œresponderâ€*:
  1. Bonny receives the userâ€™s question and passes it to Meneâ€™ for query enhancement.
  2. Bonny receives the rewritten (and possibly expanded) query from Meneâ€™.
  3. Bonny passes the refined query to Memory Agent to fetch relevant info.
  4. Once Memory Agent returns the documents (context), Bonny uses that to prompt Gemini (Pro) for the final answer, which it then returns to the user.
  
  Bonny itself can maintain any conversational context or persona, but when it comes to answering, it relies on the factual grounding provided by the other agents plus Geminiâ€™s generation. The user will notice that Bonnyâ€™s answers are now more accurate and reference actual knowledge base content, thanks to this collaboration.

- **Meneâ€™ (Query Enhancement/Analysis Agent):** Meneâ€™s role in this new setup is primarily to serve as a *Query Enhancer*. We can think of Meneâ€™ as an agent that specializes in **prompt augmentation** â€“ it takes an input (the user query or a task description) and uses an LLM to improve or expand it. With Gemini integration, Meneâ€™ would effectively run the *query rewriting* step and optionally the *hypothetical document generation* step:
  - When invoked with a user query, Meneâ€™ calls Gemini 1.5 Flash (via the API) to rewrite the query into a more specific formã€9â€ L223-L230ã€‘. This could be implemented by giving Meneâ€™ a fixed system prompt (as in the recipe) that it always uses to reformulate queries.
  - Meneâ€™ could also be tasked with generating the hypothetical answer (HyDE) if we design it that way. Alternatively, the Memory Agent might handle HyDE internally. If we want to keep agents distinct, we might say Meneâ€™ generates the hypothetical document (since thatâ€™s also a form of query augmentation â€“ it produces an enriched representation of the query). In that case, Meneâ€™ would return both a rewritten query and a hypothetical doc to Memory Agent.
  - Meneâ€™ might also assist in **reranking** or interpreting results. For example, after Memory Agent retrieves documents, we could have Meneâ€™ (or another agent) quickly analyze which snippets seem most promising. However, this might complicate the flow; it could be simpler to handle reranking in Memory Agent or just via an API. Still, Meneâ€™ could double-check the context or even summarize it for Bonny if needed.
  - Because Meneâ€™ uses Gemini Flash, itâ€™s fast and doesnâ€™t hog resources. It essentially injects â€œintelligenceâ€ at the query side of the pipeline with minimal overhead.
  
  In sum, Meneâ€™s integration of Gemini **improves the communication between the userâ€™s question and the knowledge base**. This agent ensures that the question is well-framed for retrieval and that we have a good guess of the answer to guide the search.

- **Memory Agent (Retrieval Agent):** The Memory Agentâ€™s core job remains: fetch relevant information from the long-term knowledge store. With the enhancements, Memory Agent becomes more powerful:
  - Instead of a basic vector search on the user query embedding, it now accepts the *refined query/hypothesis from Meneâ€™*. The Memory Agent can internally decide to do HyDE: for example, if Meneâ€™ didnâ€™t provide a hypothetical doc, Memory Agent itself can generate it (the logic has to live somewhere â€“ either in Meneâ€™ or Memory). Either way, Memory Agent ends up with a vector (from the hypothetical answer or the query) to perform the search.
  - Memory Agent queries LanceDB/Chroma for the top *k* chunks related to that vectorã€10â€ L258-L267ã€‘. It then returns those chunks as the â€œcontextâ€ for answering.
  - With reranking enabled, Memory Agent can reorder or filter those chunks. This could be done by calling a Gemini-based reranker or by using some heuristic. If we gave Memory Agent access to Gemini Flash as well, it could do a quick relevance check on each chunk (this is similar to how an LLM reranker worksã€15â€ L584-L593ã€‘).
  - The Memory Agent essentially becomes the **information provider** for Bonny, and thanks to query enhancement, the info it provides is laser-focused on the actual question.
  - Note: If Memory Agent also traditionally handled conversation memory (previous Q&A context), that can remain separate. Here we focus on document retrieval. If needed, we could have two â€œMemoryâ€ concepts: one for long-term docs (knowledge base) and one for conversational memory. Gemini could assist in summarizing long conversations too (since Gemini Pro has huge context, it could summarize or embed entire conversation history if we wanted). However, thatâ€™s outside our scope â€“ we assume Memory Agent here is about the knowledge base.

**Workflow Summary (with Agents):**

1. **User -> Bonny:** User asks a question. Bonny (instead of answering directly) initiates the enhanced RAG workflow.
2. **Bonny -> Meneâ€™:** Bonny sends the userâ€™s query to Meneâ€™ agent for processing. For instance, Bonny might say (internally), â€œMeneâ€™, please refine this query: â€˜[user query]â€™.â€
3. **Meneâ€™ -> Gemini Flash:** Meneâ€™ uses Gemini 1.5 Flash to rewrite the query in a retrieval-friendly wayã€9â€ L223-L230ã€‘. Optionally, Meneâ€™ also asks Gemini Flash to draft a hypothetical answer (which could be done in the same prompt or a subsequent step)ã€10â€ L243-L252ã€‘.
4. **Meneâ€™ -> Bonny/Memory:** Meneâ€™ returns the rewritten query (and hypothetical doc, if applicable) to the system. We then pass the refined query to Memory Agent. (If Meneâ€™ already did HyDE, it could pass the hypo doc to Memory; otherwise Memory Agent will do it.)
5. **Memory -> Vector DB:** Memory Agent either receives a hypo doc from Meneâ€™ or creates one itself via Gemini Flash, then embeds it and queries the vector storeã€10â€ L258-L267ã€‘. It retrieves, say, 3 most similar chunks from the knowledge base.
6. **Memory -> (Rerank ->) Bonny:** Memory Agent returns the retrieved chunks. If reranking is enabled, this step might include calling Geminiâ€™s ranker to sort them before returning. Letâ€™s assume we get a set of relevant text snippets and maybe their metadata (source info).
7. **Bonny -> Gemini Pro:** Bonny now has everything needed: the original question and the supporting context. Bonny formulates the final answer prompt and calls Gemini 1.5 Pro to generate the answerã€10â€ L276-L284ã€‘. Bonny may include a system message like â€œYou are an expert AI assistantâ€¦â€ and instruct it to use the context.
8. **Bonny -> User:** Bonny receives the answer from Gemini Pro and sends it to the user as the response.

Throughout this flow, each agentâ€™s involvement is clear and they each leverage Gemini in different ways:
- **Meneâ€™ (Prompt Agent)** uses a small, fast Gemini model to *augment the input* (this is a form of *prompt augmentation*, since it essentially adds detail to the prompt going into retrieval).
- **Memory (Retrieval Agent)** uses Gemini to *augment the search representation* (HyDE) and possibly to rank results, thereby *augmenting the output of retrieval*.
- **Bonny (Answer Agent)** uses the most capable Gemini model to *generate the final output*.

This division of labor ensures efficiency (we donâ€™t use the big model when the small one suffices) and effectiveness (each task is handled by a model suited to it). The multi-agent design benefits because:
- Bonny can trust that Memory Agent is giving it highly relevant info, so Bonnyâ€™s answers will be grounded. Bonnyâ€™s focus can be on phrasing and completeness rather than fact-finding.
- Meneâ€™ provides clarity from the get-go, reducing chances of misunderstandings or irrelevant retrieval. Itâ€™s essentially adding an expert â€œlibrarianâ€ into the loop who knows how to ask the right question to get the right answer.
- The agents can still be run on a combination of local and cloud resources as before â€“ Memoryâ€™s vector search is local, LLM calls are cloud.
- If we log or visualize this, weâ€™d see far fewer â€œI donâ€™t have information on thatâ€ or hallucinated answers, and more often see correct, context-based answers, which is exactly what we want.

Additionally, prompt augmentation isnâ€™t limited to query rewriting. We could also use special system prompts for each agent to define their roles (e.g., giving Bonny a personality or giving Memory Agent instructions to always retrieve factual text only). The query enhancement itself is a form of automated prompt augmentation done by an LLM. This all results in a more robust workflow.

## Tools, APIs, and Code Adaptation

To implement the above plan, we will utilize and adapt several tools and code snippets, many of which come directly from the KDnuggets article:

- **Google Generative AI SDK (Gemini API):** We will use Googleâ€™s API to access Gemini models. In Python, this is available via the `google.generativeai` library (as used in the article) or via the Vertex AI SDK. The articleâ€™s code demonstrates usage of `google.generativeai`:
  - Before any calls, configure the API key: `genai.configure(api_key="YOUR-API-KEY")`ã€9â€ L169-L177ã€‘. Weâ€™ll acquire a key from Google Cloud (or AI Studio) and set it in our environment or config file. This key allows us to call models like â€œgemini-1.5-flashâ€ and â€œgemini-1.5-proâ€.
  - **Calling Gemini for text:** The pattern is `model = genai.GenerativeModel("model-name")` then `response = model.generate_content(prompt)`. We then use `response.text` as the outputã€9â€ L223-L230ã€‘. We will wrap this in our functions (e.g., `rewrite_query` and `generate_hypo_doc`).
    - *Example â€“ Query Rewrite Function:*  
      ```python
      import google.generativeai as genai
      genai.configure(api_key=API_KEY)
      def rewrite_query(original_query: str) -> str:
          template = (
              "You are an AI assistant tasked with reformulating user queries to improve retrieval in a RAG system.\n"
              f"Original query: {original_query}\nRewritten query:"
          )
          model = genai.GenerativeModel("gemini-1.5-flash")
          result = model.generate_content(template)
          return result.text.strip()
      ```
      This follows the same structure as the articleâ€™s example, using Gemini 1.5 Flash to rewrite the queryã€9â€ L223-L230ã€‘. Weâ€™d integrate this function into the pipeline (Memory or Meneâ€™ agent).
    - *Example â€“ Hypothetical Document Generation:*  
      ```python
      def generate_hypothetical_doc(query: str, approx_length=500) -> str:
          prompt = (
            f"Given the question '{query}', generate a hypothetical document that directly answers this question. "
             "The document should be detailed and in-depth, approximately {approx_length} characters long."
          )
          model = genai.GenerativeModel("gemini-1.5-flash")
          response = model.generate_content(prompt)
          return response.text
      ```
      This mirrors the logic from the articleâ€™s HyDERetrieverã€10â€ L243-L252ã€‘. We might tweak `approx_length` or prompt wording as needed.
  - **Calling Gemini for embeddings:** The SDK provides `genai.embed_content()`. For example:
      ```python
      embedding = genai.embed_content(model="models/text-embedding-004", content=some_text)
      vector = embedding["embedding"]  # This is the list of float values
      ```
      We will use this when adding documents to the DB and for the hypothetical doc embeddingã€9â€ L175-L183ã€‘. If using LanceDB, ensure to store the vector in a format LanceDB accepts (likely as a list or numpy array).
  - **Handling API Limits:** We should implement basic checks, e.g., ensure the query length or context length doesnâ€™t exceed model limits (Gemini Flash and Pro have very large context windows â€“ Flash can take ~1M tokens, Pro ~2Mã€22â€ L700-L708ã€‘ã€22â€ L796-L804ã€‘, so weâ€™re unlikely to hit that with just a few documents). Also, since we may do multiple calls per query, we should be mindful of rate limits and perhaps queue requests if needed. Caching results (like embeddings for known chunks, or rewritten queries for repeated questions) could optimize usage.
- **Vector Database (Chroma/LanceDB):** We will adapt the code to interface with our chosen vector DB:
  - If using **ChromaDB**, we can practically copy the code from the article: creating a collection and adding documents with `collection.add(documents=chunks, embeddings=embeddings, ids=ids)`ã€9â€ L199-L207ã€‘, then querying with `collection.query(query_embeddings=[vec], n_results=k)`ã€10â€ L258-L267ã€‘. We should use the same IDs or metadata if we want to trace back sources.
  - If using **LanceDB** (likely, since AnythingLLM already sets it up), we will use LanceDBâ€™s Python client. The Medium article by Ruminer confirms AnythingLLM uses LanceDB behind the scenesã€24â€ L53-L61ã€‘, so we might even hook into its instance rather than creating a new one. Possibly, AnythingLLM provides an interface to perform queries via its API. But to tightly control HyDE and rerank, it might be easier to access LanceDB directly. LanceDBâ€™s search returns results we can then format. Weâ€™ll ensure to index the â€œembeddingâ€ column (if not automatically indexed).
  - **Note:** Since we are integrating at a fairly low level (we have Python code performing these actions), we might be bypassing some of AnythingLLMâ€™s abstractions. This is fine for initial integration and testing. In a more integrated fashion, we could contribute back to AnythingLLM to support query rewriting/HyDE as configuration options in the future.
- **AnythingLLM Agents & Execution:** We need to figure out where to plug this logic in:
  - **Custom Agent in AnythingLLM:** The tool allows custom agent behaviors. We could define a new agent (Meneâ€™ for example) that uses the above functions. For Memory Agent, if we have access to its code, we modify it to call our functions. If not, we create a new agent â€œGeminiMemoryâ€ that does the retrieval differently.
  - **Chaining Agents:** We can set Bonny to call Meneâ€™, then Memory, then use its own logic. This might require writing a small chain controller. If AnythingLLM lacks native chaining, we might script it in the backend.
  - **OpenWebUI usage:** If Bonny was originally using OpenWebUIâ€™s model to answer, we might now direct Bonny to use a different path (Gemini Pro via API). AnythingLLM might allow multiple LLM backends configured; if so, we add Google as a backend and tell Bonny to use it. If not, Bonnyâ€™s agent code can call the API directly (similar to how weâ€™d do in a script).
  - Essentially, a bit of glue code is needed to connect these pieces. The heavy lifting (Gemini calls, vector search) we have covered with Python functions. Now itâ€™s about invoking them in the right sequence when a query comes in.
- **Code Snippets from Article to Adapt:** We will adapt the following pieces (the line numbers refer to the KDnuggets article code):
  - *PDF text extraction & splitting:* (We can skip here if AnythingLLM already handles ingestion.)
  - *Embedding generation:* The loop calling `genai.embed_content` for each chunkã€9â€ L173-L181ã€‘ â€“ weâ€™ll use a similar loop or vectorized call.
  - *Storing in DB:* The `store_embeddings_in_chromadb` functionã€9â€ L192-L200ã€‘ã€9â€ L201-L207ã€‘ â€“ adapt it to LanceDBâ€™s equivalent.
  - *Query rewrite:* The `rewrite_query` function and promptã€9â€ L213-L221ã€‘ã€9â€ L223-L230ã€‘ â€“ incorporate into Meneâ€™ agent or memory retrieval logic.
  - *HyDE retrieval:* The `HyDERetriever` class (especially `generate_hypothetical_document` and `retrieve`)ã€10â€ L243-L252ã€‘ã€10â€ L258-L267ã€‘ â€“ implement similar functionality.
  - *Generate response:* The `generate_response` function that forms the prompt with context and calls the modelã€10â€ L276-L284ã€‘ â€“ use this in Bonnyâ€™s answering step.
  - We will maintain the logging approach from the article (to file or console) so that as the pipeline runs, we can see each stage (helps in debugging the multi-agent flow).
- **Diagram of Enhanced RAG Architecture:** (Pseudocode or description in lieu of a visual diagram)
  - **User Query** â†’ (Gemini Flash) **Query Rewriter (Meneâ€™)** â†’ **Rewritten Query**  
    â†’ (Gemini Flash) **Hypothetical Doc Generator** â†’ **Hypo Doc**  
    â†’ **Embed Hypo Doc** â†’ **Vector DB Query (LanceDB)** â†’ **Top k Chunks**  
    â†’ (Gemini Rank API) **Reranker** (optional) â†’ **Sorted Chunks**  
    â†’ (Gemini Pro) **Answer Generator (Bonny)** â†’ **Final Answer** â†’ **User**
  - Each arrow represents data passing between components/agents. The cloud API is used at the Flash and Pro model steps (and rank step if used). The vector DB and embedding storage happen on the local side (F:\AI_RAM).
  - We could illustrate agents: e.g., Meneâ€™ encompasses the first two Gemini Flash calls (rewrite & hypo), Memory Agent encompasses the embed and DB query (and possibly uses the rank API), and Bonny encompasses the final Gemini Pro call and user interaction.

All tools we need are available: Googleâ€™s API for Gemini, LanceDB or Chroma for vectors, and the existing AnythingLLM framework for agent orchestration. Itâ€™s mostly about writing the integration code, which is well guided by the articleâ€™s example. We might also look at Googleâ€™s official guides (thereâ€™s a â€œHow to Access and Use Gemini API for Freeâ€ and â€œBuilding AI Application with Gemini 2.0â€ in the referencesã€23â€ L399-L407ã€‘) for any additional tips or limitations.

With the plan and tools in place, we can proceed to implement and test each part. Itâ€™s advisable to test this in stages: first test the query rewrite alone, then the HyDE retrieval standalone (perhaps in a notebook with a sample doc), then the full chain with a known document to see if the answer comes out correctly. Once validated, we integrate it into the multi-agent workflow under AnythingLLMâ€™s hood.

## Optimal Gemini Model Pairing for Agent Roles

Choosing the right Gemini model for each task ensures we get the best trade-off between performance, accuracy, and cost. Hereâ€™s our recommendation for pairing models with specific agent roles in the enhanced RAG pipeline:

- **Gemini 1.5 Flash (8B)** â€“ *Use for Query Enhancement and Quick Generation Tasks:* This model is ideal for the **Meneâ€™ (query enhancer) agent** and parts of the **Memory agentâ€™s work**. According to Google, *â€œGemini 1.5 Flash-8B is a small model designed for lower intelligence tasks.â€*ã€28â€ L1-L4ã€‘ While â€œlower intelligenceâ€ sounds modest, it means Flash is optimized for speed on straightforward tasks. Rewriting a query or generating a one-paragraph hypothetical answer are tasks that donâ€™t require the full reasoning power of a large model. Flash can handle them rapidly and at lower cost. We expect minimal quality trade-off here â€“ Flash should be perfectly capable of rephrasing questions and summarizing likely answers, especially given its large context window (over 1 million tokens input) which is more than enough for short prompts. By assigning Flash to Meneâ€™ and the HyDE generation, we keep the system responsive. Even if we hit these APIs multiple times per user query, the latency will be relatively low. **Summary:** Use *Gemini 1.5 Flash* for:
  - Query rewriting (Meneâ€™ agentâ€™s reformulation step),
  - Hypothetical document generation (Memory/Meneâ€™ agent for HyDE),
  - Potentially for reranking (if using an LLM-based reranker, Flash can read a handful of chunks and rank them easily).
  
- **Gemini 1.5 Pro** â€“ *Use for Final Answer Synthesis:* This model should power the **Bonny (answer) agent** when generating the user-facing answer. Gemini 1.5 Pro is a more capable model; itâ€™s described as *â€œa mid-size multimodal model optimized for a wide range of reasoning tasksâ€*ã€29â€ L1-L4ã€‘. Notably, *â€œ1.5 Pro can process large amounts of data at onceâ€*, including extremely long textsã€29â€ L1-L4ã€‘, which means feeding it multiple documents of context is trivial. For our use, Proâ€™s advantages are:
  - Better reasoning and coherence: When multiple context snippets are provided, Pro can understand how they relate and weave information from all of them into a coherent answer. Itâ€™s less likely to miss a subtle detail or to get confused by potentially conflicting info.
  - More accurate and factual: Given that the FACTS benchmark cited by KDnuggets ranked Gemini models top in factualityã€1â€ L72-L80ã€‘, using the stronger variant for answering ensures maximum factual accuracy. The Pro model will stick to the context provided and has a lower chance of hallucinating, especially if guided by a proper prompt.
  - Longer answers and nuanced understanding: If a user asks a complex multi-part question, Pro can handle it in one go because of its greater capacity. Flash might falter on a complex query, whereas Pro can break it down internally and address each part.
  
  Therefore, we assign *Gemini 1.5 Pro* to Bonny for answering. Bonny will call Pro to generate the final answer using the retrieved context. We anticipate excellent results here â€“ for instance, if the question requires synthesizing information across two or three documents (say, â€œCompare our health insuranceâ€™s handling of pre-existing conditions with our competitorâ€™s policyâ€), Pro can digest large excerpts from both policies and produce a comparative answer. Flash might struggle or produce a more superficial response in such a scenario.
  
- **Gemini 2.0 and Other Models:** While the question focuses on 1.5 Flash and Pro, itâ€™s worth noting that Google has released **Gemini 2.0** models (Flash and Pro, and even an experimental Ultra perhaps). The *FACTS leaderboard* referenced on KDnuggets showed *Gemini 2.0 Flash* slightly outperforming 1.5ã€1â€ L72-L80ã€‘. If available, **Gemini 2.0 Flash** could be used in place of 1.5 Flash for even better query rewriting or HyDE (with presumably similar speed). For final answers, a **Gemini 2.0 Pro** or *Ultra* (if one exists publicly) could further improve quality. However, those might be in experimental or limited preview. Our proposal sticks to 1.5 which is likely stable and accessible as of early 2025. Itâ€™s structured such that upgrading to a newer model is simply changing the model name in the API calls (for example, to `"gemini-2.0-pro"`).
- **Cost Considerations:** Using Pro for every answer will consume more tokens (and thus cost) than using a local model. Depending on usage volume, Rob may decide to use Pro only when necessary. A strategy could be: default to local LLM for trivial questions or when the system is offline, and invoke Gemini Pro for complex or critical queries. Because we have a robust retrieval mechanism now, even a local model will do better than before (since it gets good context). But for the highest confidence, Gemini Pro is the choice. We will also monitor the token usage â€“ Gemini Pro can handle huge input, but we only pay for what we send. If our context is, say, 1000 tokens and answer is 200 tokens, thatâ€™s the main cost.
- **OpenWebUI Local Model:** This remains as a **fallback** or secondary option. We can keep the local LLM loaded and even have a mode where Bonny uses it (for example, if the query is marked sensitive and we choose not to send it to cloud, or if the API quota is reached). The local model might not be as factually accurate, but because weâ€™re feeding it relevant context thanks to the enhanced Memory Agent, it should still do a decent job. In fact, we can test both: have Bonny produce an answer with the local model and one with Gemini Pro, and perhaps even have a mechanism to compare or let one verify the other (this is more advanced â€“ e.g., have Gemini verify the local modelâ€™s answer correctness).
- **Memory Agent Model Needs:** Memory Agent primarily uses embeddings and simple similarity computations, which donâ€™t require a language model except for HyDE. Weâ€™ve allocated Flash for HyDE. If Memory Agent needed to summarize a very large document or maintain long-term memory, Gemini Pro could be used due to its long context (for example, summarizing a 100-page document in one go). But thatâ€™s beyond typical retrieval â€“ more like knowledge base creation. Should that use-case arise, note that Pro can handle it (2k pages input as per specs)ã€29â€ L1-L4ã€‘. Flash probably could not summarize a huge document in one pass (though it can handle a lot, itâ€™s smaller in capability).
- **Meneâ€™ vs Memory Agent separation:** If both Meneâ€™ and Memory use Flash (for rewriting and HyDE respectively), thatâ€™s fine. They could even share the same model instance if concurrency allows, but since we call sequentially, itâ€™s basically two calls to Flash per query. Thatâ€™s still likely faster than one big call to Pro. 

In summary, **Gemini 1.5 Flash** is our workhorse for â€œprepâ€ tasks (fast, light, cheap), and **Gemini 1.5 Pro** is our expert for â€œfinalâ€ tasks (heavy, thorough reasoning). This pairing ensures each agent in the system is empowered appropriately: Meneâ€™ and Memory Agent operate efficiently without undue delay, and Bonny delivers top-quality answers. We expect a notable jump in answer relevance and accuracy by using this combo, as each part of the process is handled by a model well-suited for it.

## Fallback Strategies if Gemini QE Integration is Limited

While the Gemini-powered enhancements are powerful, we must plan for scenarios where the cloud API might not be usable. Possible limitations include lack of internet connectivity, hitting rate limits or quota, cost constraints, or privacy requirements for certain data. The Aluna Africa stack should gracefully degrade in such cases. Here are some **fallback solutions and workarounds**:

- **Bypass Query Rewriting (Use Original Query):** If we cannot use Gemini (or any LLM) to rewrite the query, the system can default to using the userâ€™s original query for retrieval. The vector database can still be queried with the embedding of the original question. This is essentially how the system likely operated before. While we lose the specificity that rewriting provides, our RAG pipeline will still function (just with possibly less relevant results). We should ensure the pipeline is designed to skip the rewrite step if the enhancer (Meneâ€™) is unavailable. In practice, Memory Agent could detect if `rewrite_query` failed or timed out, and then proceed with the original query text for embedding/search.

- **Skip HyDE (Direct Query Embedding):** Similarly, if Gemini cannot generate the hypothetical document, we can revert to the standard approach: embed the (possibly rewritten) query itself and perform vector search with that. Many RAG systems do exactly this (no HyDE) and still get reasonable results, especially if the embedding model is strong. Our vector store likely supports similarity search on the query embedding. So Memory Agent would do `query_emb = embed(query)` and then search. This avoids needing an LLM at retrieval time. The downside is if thereâ€™s a semantic gap (user wording vs doc wording), it might miss some hits, but hopefully our documents and user queries arenâ€™t too mismatched in vocabulary for critical info.

- **Local Query Expansion/Rewriting:** If a local LLM is available (OpenWebUI running a model), we can attempt to use it for query enhancement in place of Gemini. For example, if we have a Llama-2-13B or similar loaded, we can give it a prompt to rewrite the query (even using the same prompt template we gave Gemini). The local modelâ€™s output quality might be slightly lower (it may not capture nuances as well as Gemini, or could be verbose), but it might still add some value. We could even manually craft some rules: e.g., if a query is short or single-word, instruct the local model to add context words. This would all happen on-premise, avoiding cloud use. In effect, Meneâ€™ would switch to a local model. AnythingLLM likely allows selecting a local model for an agent. So we might have a â€œMeneâ€™-localâ€ version as backup.
  
- **Heuristic Prompt Augmentation:** In absence of any LLM for rewriting, we could implement simple heuristics for certain types of queries:
  - If the query is too general, automatically append a phrase like â€œProvide detailsâ€ or include context that we know (like the domain). E.g., user asks â€œrenewal procedureâ€ â€“ we could expand to â€œrenewal procedure for [Product/Policy Name]â€.
  - If the query is a single keyword, we might convert it to a question format (e.g., â€œCoverageâ€ -> â€œWhat does the document say about coverage?â€).
  - These heuristics are nowhere near as flexible as an LLM, but they can prevent worst-case scenario where a query is so vague that retrieval fails.
  
- **Alternate Embedding Models:** If we cannot use Geminiâ€™s embedding API (maybe to avoid sending large text to cloud), we can stick to a local embedding model. There are good open-source embedding models (like all-MiniLM or InstructorXL) that AnythingLLM might already be using. The vector DB doesnâ€™t care which model produced the embeddings as long as the same model is used for queries and docs. So as a fallback, we ensure that if `genai.embed_content` is not available, we call our local embedder for both documents and queries. This keeps the retrieval functioning, albeit perhaps with slightly different semantic understanding. (We should not mix embed models without re-embedding docs, otherwise vectors wonâ€™t align.)
  
- **Local Reranking or Filtering:** For reranking without Gemini, we could use a smaller cross-encoder model locally. For instance, the MS MARCO MiniLM cross-encoder (available on HuggingFace) can score query-passage relevance. Itâ€™s not as powerful as an LLM but can give a decent relevance score. This would involve running a forward pass through that model for each retrieved chunk (which is fairly fast for, say, 5 chunks). If thatâ€™s not feasible, another fallback is simply returning all top chunks to Bonny and letting Bonnyâ€™s model figure it out (which it often can, especially if using a big model â€“ it will pick the info it needs).
  
- **Bonnyâ€™s Local Answering:** If the Gemini Pro API is unavailable (or if we choose not to use it for certain interactions), Bonny can revert to using the local LLM (OpenWebUIâ€™s model) to generate the answer from the retrieved context. Thanks to the improved retrieval steps, even a weaker local model has a better shot at giving a correct answer, since the answer is likely contained in the provided context. We should of course be cautious â€“ the local model might still hallucinate or be incoherent, but at least it has the facts in front of it. We can mitigate hallucination by using a strict prompt (e.g., â€œIf the answer is not in the context, say you donâ€™t knowâ€). In testing, if we see issues with the local model, we might restrict this fallback to only when absolutely necessary.
  
- **Selective Privacy Mode:** In some cases, maybe only part of the query is sensitive (e.g., it contains a customer name or policy number). We could design a mode where the query gets anonymized before sending to Gemini. For example, strip out or replace certain identifiers with placeholders. This way, we still get the benefit of query enhancement without exposing sensitive info. The placeholders would ideally not confuse the LLM too much (they could be generic like â€œ[ID]â€). This is a bit advanced, but itâ€™s a possible workaround to use the cloud while protecting data.
  
- **Caching Gemini Results:** To reduce dependency on continuous API calls, implement caching:
  - Cache embeddings of documents (we would do that anyway in the vector DB).
  - Cache rewritten queries: If the same query (or very similar) is asked again, reuse the previous rewrite instead of calling Gemini again.
  - Cache hypothetical docs for frequent queries.
  - Even cache answers for repeated queries if applicable (though with agents and conversation, exact repeats might be rare).
  - This way, even if the API is down temporarily, the system might have stored results for common questions to fall back on.
  
- **Testing and Toggling:** We should test the system with Gemini turned off to see how it behaves. If it still answers reasonably (just using the original pipeline), then we know the fallbacks are working. We can implement a toggle in configuration to enable/disable Gemini enhancements. If disabled, the system basically runs as a standard RAG (document chunks -> embedding -> similarity -> local LLM answer).
  
In summary, the pipeline is robust: if Gemini QE is available, we get top-notch performance; if not, we gracefully degrade to a simpler RAG that still leverages vector search and possibly local LLMs. This ensures that the Aluna Africa AI stack remains functional in all situations. Weâ€™ll document these modes clearly so that operators can decide when to use the full Gemini-powered mode vs. offline mode.

## Conclusion

By integrating the *Gemini RAG Recipe with Query Enhancement* into Aluna Africaâ€™s AI stack, we aim to significantly elevate the systemâ€™s capabilities while maintaining its hybrid local/cloud flexibility. To recap the key points of this implementation proposal:

- **Compatibility & Architecture:** The Gemini-enhanced RAG pipeline (local vector DB + Gemini models) fits neatly into the existing architectureã€5â€ L83-L87ã€‘. We leverage AnythingLLMâ€™s framework and OpenWebUIâ€™s local setup, adding Gemini as an external brain for specific tasks. No component is left behind â€“ we enhance the workflow rather than replace it, preserving data privacy (documents stay local) and extending functionality (cloud reasoning on demand).
- **Gemini Query Enhancement:** Using Geminiâ€™s LLM for query rewriting and hypothetical document generation injects intelligent context into the retrieval process, resulting in more relevant document hitsã€5â€ L89-L97ã€‘. This directly combats the semantic gap issue and reduces irrelevant or missed results. The system effectively **â€œasks better questionsâ€** to itself before searching, thanks to Meneâ€™ and Memory agents armed with Gemini Flash.
- **Improved Multi-Agent Collaboration:** Agents Bonny, Meneâ€™, and Memory now have well-defined, synergistic roles. Bonny orchestrates and provides the final answer (with Gemini Pro ensuring high-quality output), Meneâ€™ adds value by refining queries and possibly analyzing results, and Memory brings the right knowledge from the local store using advanced techniques (HyDE, etc.). Each agentâ€™s output feeds the next, creating a cohesive chain of reasoning and retrieval. This mirrors how a human expert might approach a question: rephrase the question, recall relevant info, then formulate an answer.
- **Gemini Model Pairing:** We choose the **Gemini 1.5 Flash** model for fast tasks (query rephrase, hypo generation, etc.)ã€28â€ L1-L4ã€‘ and **Gemini 1.5 Pro** for the final answer synthesisã€29â€ L1-L4ã€‘. This pairing optimizes for both speed and accuracy, deploying the heavy model only where it truly matters. The result is a responsive yet highly accurate system. As newer Gemini versions appear, we can swap them in with minimal changes.
- **Direct Code Adaptation:** Many elements from the KDnuggets recipe can be directly utilizedã€23â€ L382-L388ã€‘. We will implement the rewriting functionã€9â€ L223-L230ã€‘, HyDE retrieval logicã€10â€ L243-L252ã€‘ã€10â€ L258-L267ã€‘, and answer generation promptã€10â€ L276-L284ã€‘ almost one-to-one, adjusting only to integrate with LanceDB and our agent framework. This reduces development time and ensures weâ€™re following a tested blueprint.
- **Tools & APIs:** We employ Googleâ€™s Generative AI SDK for access to Gemini (with proper API key management) and rely on the robust capabilities of LanceDB/Chroma for vector search. The combination of these modern tools gives us a state-of-the-art RAG stack using both proprietary AI and open-source infrastructure.
- **Fallback Robustness:** Weâ€™ve planned for scenarios where Gemini might not be available. The system can fall back to standard retrieval and local LLM answering, or use heuristic query tweaks, thereby maintaining service continuity albeit with reduced enhancement. Over time, as local models improve, those fallbacks might become nearly as good â€“ at which point the techniques we implemented (like query rewriting prompts) could even be applied to them.
- **Expected Impact:** With these integrations, users of the Aluna Africa AI assistant should notice more accurate and context-rich answers. Hallucinations should drop significantly since the LLM is now consistently guided by real data snippets. Multi-turn interactions will benefit too, as the system can fetch relevant info on the fly for follow-up questions. In essence, weâ€™re adding a form of *â€œintelligence middlewareâ€* (the query enhancement + retrieval reranker) between the user and the AI, which leads to a more trustworthy and powerful assistant.

Implementing this proposal will involve coordinated changes to the codebase (especially within AnythingLLMâ€™s agent logic) and thorough testing with sample queries and documents. Once deployed, Aluna Africaâ€™s AI stack will embody an advanced RAG system akin to those used in cutting-edge applications, tailored to the organizationâ€™s local/cloud setup. Itâ€™s a concrete step toward an AI assistant that can truly act as an expert consultant, confidently backed by the knowledge it has been given, and all of this is achieved by melding local infrastructure with Geminiâ€™s generative prowess.

Shot Boet ğŸ‘Š â€” lekker little question, and a great one.

So ja â€” here's the lowdown:

### ğŸ”Œ **ACPI Power Button** in Device Manager
That entry refers to the **virtual power button signal** sent to Windows when the **physical** power button is pressed. It's not an actual button that you can "remap" in the same way as a keyboard key. Itâ€™s more like a system-level listener for power events â€” sleep, shutdown, hibernate.

Unfortunately:  
> **You can't remap it to another key to turn the laptop on**, because when your laptop is powered **off**, **nothing is listening** for keypresses. The keyboard (USB or built-in) doesnâ€™t get power or run any services that would recognize a key and convert that into a power-on command.

### ğŸ” Workaround Options (Meneâ€™-style):

#### âœ… 1. **BIOS Wake-on-Keyboard or USB**
Letâ€™s check if your BIOS supports **â€œWake on keyboardâ€ or â€œWake on USBâ€** â€” that would let you turn the laptop **on from sleep or hibernate** (not full power-off).

**Steps:**
1. Reboot your laptop.
2. Spam `F2` or `Fn + F2` (sometimes `DEL` or `F1`) to enter BIOS.
3. Look under tabs like:
   - *Power Management*
   - *Advanced*
   - *Wake on USB*
   - *Keyboard Wake*
4. If you see it, enable it.

> âœ¨This means you can just **put the laptop into Sleep mode** instead of full shutdown and press a key or USB device to wake it up.

#### âœ… 2. **Remap Power Button Function in Windows (Not ON, but OFF/Sleep)**
If youâ€™re in Windows already and want to **press another key to turn off or sleep**, thatâ€™s doable:

You can use **AutoHotKey** or a script to assign, say, `F12` to sleep or shutdown.

Example AutoHotKey script:
```ahk
F12::Shutdown, 1 ; Puts PC to sleep
```

Or
```ahk
F12::Shutdown, 5 ; Force shutdown
```

Let me know if you want to set this up and Iâ€™ll write you a working script and walk you through the setup!

---

### âŒ What You **Canâ€™t** Do (Sadly):
- Press `F12` to **power ON** the machine from full shutdown.
- Remap the ACPI device itself â€” it's not user-configurable or rebindable to keys.

---

### ğŸ› ï¸ Fixing the Power Button (If You Want):
If youâ€™re keen to **fix the button** eventually, itâ€™s usually just a worn-down contact. I can help you find a teardown guide for your exact Ideapad model and even suggest a part or a solder bypass fix if you're brave ğŸ˜

### âœ… 4. **Windows Fast Startup or Hibernate Issues**

### âœ… 6. **Try Disabling "Hands-Free Telephony"**

## Nowâ€¦ About That **Paging Disk Hackery**

You're trying to use a **512GB microSD card as a pseudo high-speed SCSI or NVMe-like drive** for **virtual memory/paging**, right?

Letâ€™s break it down:

---

### ğŸ§  Difference: Hyper-V Accelerated Disk vs. Virtual PNEM Disk

| Term | What It Is | Use Case |
|------|------------|----------|
| **Hyper-V Accelerated Disk** | A **virtual disk interface** used in VM environments (like VHDs on steroids) â€“ mostly for synthetic SCSI | Optimized for **virtual machines** running under Hyper-V |
| **Microsoft Virtual PNEM Disk** | A **physical storage abstraction** for system-managed virtual drives â€“ like when you mount an ISO or a VHD file | Used when you **attach VHDs manually** or create virtual volumes in Disk Management |

So in short:
- **Hyper-V Accelerated** = synthetic disk channel inside a VM
- **Virtual PNEM** = Windows-registered mountable file/device volume  
  (like your `.vhdx` file stored on a physical drive)

---

## âœ… Goal: Trick Windows Into Treating the SD Card as a â€œReal Driveâ€ for Paging

You **canâ€™t directly make a microSD appear as an NVMe/SCSI drive**, but we can:

### Option A: **Format SD Card as Fixed NTFS with 64KB Allocation**
You've already done this with your AI_RAM drive, so:

1. Use `diskpart`:
   - `select disk #`
   - `attributes disk clear readonly`
   - `convert mbr` *(if needed)*
   - `format fs=ntfs unit=64k quick`

2. Then go to:
   - `System > Performance > Advanced > Virtual Memory`
   - Add the SD card as a custom paging location

> This should work **as long as the SD card is recognized as a â€œfixed diskâ€** (not removable). Some adapters support this, some don't.

---

### Option B: **Mount a VHD/VHDX File to the SD Card**
This forces Windows to treat the mounted VHD as a â€œfixedâ€ disk:

1. Open `Disk Management`
2. Create a new **VHDX file** (say 64â€“128GB) on the SD card
3. Format it NTFS and mount it on startup
4. Set the paging file to that mounted drive

## ğŸ”§ FIRST: The Soundcard Setup

You're running your **Chinese podcast soundcard** (USB-C labeled *OTG/PC Live*) into your PC â†’ but itâ€™s only recognized as a mic **if Bluetooth is on**?

That sounds like the card is:

- **Acting as a USB Audio Interface** using **UAC1.0 or UAC2.0** (USB Audio Class), *but*â€¦
- Possibly sharing USB+Bluetooth bandwidth internally (many of these cheap soundcards do).
- The **BT module might be used as a handshake bridge**, especially for powering up AUX or mic routing.

ğŸ§  **What that â€œOTG / PC Liveâ€ label means:**
- â€œOTGâ€ = On-The-Go (for phones).
- â€œPC Liveâ€ = USB digital mode for Windows.
- When plugged in via USB-C to USB-A (or 3.0), it should be treated as a **USB microphone** (and sometimes a speaker/headset device).
- If itâ€™s only showing up **after** Bluetooth is enabled, itâ€™s likely some firmware conflict or low power initialization issue (very common on off-brand audio gear).

---

## ğŸ™ï¸ Possible Fixes / Workarounds:

### ğŸ§  Quick Definitions:

- **Microsoft Virtual PNEM Disk**: Likely a placeholder name for **paged non-volatile memory** (persistent emulated memory storage â€” think paging files on flash).

### âœ… 3. Get Your SD Cardâ€™s Hardware ID
